<div id="review_1561873165" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1561873165" />
    <a title="Mogsy (MMOGC)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4379514-mogsy-mmogc"><img alt="Mogsy (MMOGC)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1286325553p2/4379514.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1561873165?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mogsy (MMOGC)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mogsy (MMOGC)" href="/user/show/4379514-mogsy-mmogc">Mogsy (MMOGC)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781447220039" href="/book/show/15946688-jaws">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4379514-mogsy-mmogc?shelf=animals">animals</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4379514-mogsy-mmogc?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4379514-mogsy-mmogc?shelf=thriller-suspense">thriller-suspense</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4379514-mogsy-mmogc?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1561873165" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17075687797507456038">3.5 of 5 stars at The BiblioSanctum <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://bibliosanctum.com/2016/03/24/backlist-burndown/'>http://bibliosanctum.com/2016/03/24/b...</a><br/><br/>A whole generation was scared off from swimming in the ocean by the Spielberg film based on this book. Embarrassingly, I have to say my own reaction was even more extreme. It was the early 90s and I must have been about 7 when I watched Jaws for the first time on VHS, and for an entire week I refused baths because I was terrified little great whites were going to pour out of the faucets and eat my face. I was an especial</span>
  <span id="freeText17075687797507456038" style="display:none">3.5 of 5 stars at The BiblioSanctum <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bibliosanctum.com/2016/03/24/backlist-burndown/">http://bibliosanctum.com/2016/03/24/b...</a><br /><br />A whole generation was scared off from swimming in the ocean by the Spielberg film based on this book. Embarrassingly, I have to say my own reaction was even more extreme. It was the early 90s and I must have been about 7 when I watched Jaws for the first time on VHS, and for an entire week I refused baths because I was terrified little great whites were going to pour out of the faucets and eat my face. I was an especially wimpy kid with an overactive imagination.<br /><br />Anyway, fast forward more than ten years, because that was how long it took before I finally managed to screw up the courage to watch the movie again. By then, I was in college and had forgotten much of what happened in the story, so aside from my memories of a couple <s>horrific</s> iconic scenes that have forever burned themselves into the hard drives of my mind, in many ways it was almost like seeing it for the first time all over again. The difference was, I was no longer a child. And chalk it up to the impatience of my twenty-something-year-old self or the fact that the movie was already more than 30 years old by that point, I realized then how needlessly I’d hyped that experience up for myself. Watching Jaws through fresh eyes, it occurred to me that the movie was actually kind of…boring.<br /><br />But don’t get me wrong; I’ve certainly come to love the film now that I’m older, because I obviously wouldn’t have bothered to check out the book it was adapted from if I wasn’t such a big fan. So, why have I rambled on and on about movie in this review so far when, really, I should have been discussing the Peter Benchley novel instead? Well, it’s because a lot of things because clearer to me after I read this. Let’s face it, barring a handful of edge-of-your-seat moments in the beginning of the film and of course John Williams’ classic score, things don’t really get going until Brody, Hooper and Quint finally end up on the ocean to hunt that big damn shark. Up until that point, much of it was terribly long and terribly dry, and if I thought that about the slow burn build-up of the movie, a part of me couldn’t help but wonder how I was going to make out with the source material.<br /><br />Truth is, I ended up being pleasantly surprised. The book kept me thoroughly entertained from start to finish, and not only on account of the differences from the movie. It’s clear to me now that a faithful adaptation wouldn’t have worked at all, because of the much deeper, more profound themes in the novel—which I hadn’t expected at all. Benchley must also have realized that writing a horror/suspense-thriller book about a man-eating shark wasn’t going to be easy, if nothing else because every scene on land was going to require a little something extra. After all, no ocean means no shark, and no shark means no action. In other words, boring.<br /><br />So, not surprisingly, actual scenes with the shark—or “the fish”, as it was called in this book—were written with this cold and almost detached attitude, leaving readers with no illusions as to its brutal nature, and when it kills, you can bet there’s no skimping on the blood and gore.<br /><br />But hey, what about when the story isn’t focused on the shark? Well, as a matter of fact, plenty of other things happen, including Mayor Vaughn’s connections to the mafia, and a torrid affair between Brody’s wife and Hooper. Ellen Brody, who was barely an afterthought in the movie, is actually a central character in the novel with a major storyline surrounding her intense longing for the affluent life she led before she got pregnant by Brody, which is why she ended up marrying him and settling in Amity. The overall feel of the book is undeniably more melancholy and mature.<br /><br />On the flip side, the darker tone meant that we lost much of the bromance that made the movie so enjoyable towards the end, and the characters were all so thoroughly unappealing that more than once I ended up rooting for the shark. The finale was also nowhere near as explosive or satisfying, so ultimately, I think it’s safe to say that while the book wins in some areas, it also loses spectacularly in others.<br /><br />Still, I have to say reading Peter Benchley’s Jaws was more enjoyable than I thought it would be, especially for an older book that’s so inherently associated with its popular adaptation. I’m guessing if you’re interested in checking it out, it’s because you’re like me—a fan of the movie who was really curious to see what in the novel made it in, what got changed, and what got cut. If you want to get the full picture, this is definitely a must-read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17075687797507456038" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1476343114" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15504955659881565847"><b>3.5 stars</b><br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1478632992ra/21094255.gif' width='500' height='215' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Jaws was such a pleasant surprise for me. I always loved the movie, and the novel is so much more than just your average <i>monster book</i>. <br/><br/>I was completely amazed by the characters' depth, and how utterly unlikeable most of them are. Ellen Brody is some sort of modern Emma Bovary, while Harper is a jackass. <br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1471411550ra/20045475.gif' width='500' height='265' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>Not to mention the mayor who got involved with the wrong kind of people. And because of that, he is willing to let innocent people die for profit.<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1478631676ra/21094030.gif' width='500' height='220' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I like the omniscient point of view. Thi</span>
  <span id="freeText15504955659881565847" style="display:none"><b>3.5 stars</b><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1478632992ra/21094255.gif" width="500" height="215" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Jaws was such a pleasant surprise for me. I always loved the movie, and the novel is so much more than just your average <i>monster book</i>. <br><br>I was completely amazed by the characters' depth, and how utterly unlikeable most of them are. Ellen Brody is some sort of modern Emma Bovary, while Harper is a jackass. <br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1471411550ra/20045475.gif" width="500" height="265" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Not to mention the mayor who got involved with the wrong kind of people. And because of that, he is willing to let innocent people die for profit.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1478631676ra/21094030.gif" width="500" height="220" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I like the omniscient point of view. This way we get to peek inside the shark's <i>mind</i>. Well, the author acknowledges it's not much of a mind, but it's still nice to know the fish's POV.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1478633148i/21094276.gif" width="500" height="375" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I listened to the audiobook and I highly recommend it. </span>

<div id="review_2190397583" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2190397583" />
    <a title="Jonathan Janz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4415785-jonathan-janz"><img alt="Jonathan Janz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1518203259p2/4415785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2190397583?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan Janz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan Janz" href="/user/show/4415785-jonathan-janz">Jonathan Janz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2190397583" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5363058567061454469">I get no pleasure from writing negative reviews. I have no problem with others who rip books to shreds, but maybe because I'm a teacher and see in an author a former child now working tirelessly to pursue a lifelong dream, I feel a little guilty criticizing others too harshly. <br/><br/>So please know that I dread saying this, but...<br/><br/>JAWS isn't a very good book. <br/><br/>I take that back--it's a wildly uneven book. Peter Benchley absolutely nailed the shark attack scenes; I mean that sincerely. The moments leading </span>
  <span id="freeText5363058567061454469" style="display:none">I get no pleasure from writing negative reviews. I have no problem with others who rip books to shreds, but maybe because I'm a teacher and see in an author a former child now working tirelessly to pursue a lifelong dream, I feel a little guilty criticizing others too harshly. <br /><br />So please know that I dread saying this, but...<br /><br />JAWS isn't a very good book. <br /><br />I take that back--it's a wildly uneven book. Peter Benchley absolutely nailed the shark attack scenes; I mean that sincerely. The moments leading up to and during the attacks were riveting, pulse-pounding, nail-biting. I could scarcely breathe whenever the shark would appear. <br /><br />So what happened to the rest? <br /><br />The key, I think, lies in this Robert Shaw quote (himself a novelist and the man who turned Quint--a chilling but one-note psychopath in the book--into one of the most iconic characters in film history): "JAWS was not a novel. It was a story written by a committee, a piece of shit." <br /><br />Spoilers ahead. I guess. <br /><br />Maybe I just want to think well of Benchley, who was quite knowledgable about sharks, and who--if the attack scenes were indeed penned by him--could truly write. I want to imagine that the Ellen Brody/Matt Hooper affair was imposed upon him by the publisher; that the endless, clumsy info dumps resulted from a meddling co-writer not trusting the reader as much as Benchley did; that the implausible character motivations and the eye-rolling deus ex machina were attributable to someone other than the author. <br /><br />Whatever the case, the book is notable only for providing just enough raw material for better storytellers to transform it into one of the greatest movies ever made. <br /><br />The film version is a true classic. It's not hyperbole to say that JAWS is a perfect film. <br /><br />Thank goodness that Spielberg jettisoned the affair and instead allowed Hooper to be written and played with nuance and immense likability. Thank goodness Spielberg and screenwriter Carl Gottlieb (and the uncredited, extraordinarily talented scribe Howard Sackler) transformed Brody into something other than an ineffectual cuckold. And man, the difference between Quint the sadistic book character (what he does to young sharks, admittedly, is shocking and haunting) and Robert Shaw's modern-day Ahab is like the difference between a tuna and a great white. <br /><br />So read the book if you want a few jolts of real adrenaline. I'm not kidding. The action scenes are spectacular. <br /><br />But if you want the other elements of a good story--you know, like characters you don't want pureed and used as chum--check out a different book. <br /><br />Or just rewatch JAWS. I promise you, it never gets old.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5363058567061454469" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12675361596396949067" style="display:none">Jaws or 'the fish' as he prefers to be known is one big ass, mean motherfucking, rip toothed, man eating great white shark whose sole job is to harass the swimmers at Amity beach to the point where no-one dare go in the water and the livelihood of everyone in the town is severely under threat.<br /> <br />So what makes him perfectly qualified for this role, well he's got big fucking teeth, swims like a torpedo and has developed a taste for reading swimmers, boats and fishermen. <span id="freeTextContainer11731312511595518890">Jaws the movie,was the first of Hollywood's summer blockbusters. I remember it playing it for a good long while,over and over in Pakistani cinemas. <br/><br/>A small seaside resort community is terrorized by a man eating shark. The shark has to be hunted down and the question is whether or not the beach should be declared off limits to all visitors. But if that is done,the resort's economy would collapse.<br/><br/>The book has its share of subplots including marital infidelity,which were omitted in the movie. <br/><br/>It's </span>
  <span id="freeText11731312511595518890" style="display:none">Jaws the movie,was the first of Hollywood's summer blockbusters. I remember it playing it for a good long while,over and over in Pakistani cinemas. <br /><br />A small seaside resort community is terrorized by a man eating shark. The shark has to be hunted down and the question is whether or not the beach should be declared off limits to all visitors. But if that is done,the resort's economy would collapse.<br /><br />The book has its share of subplots including marital infidelity,which were omitted in the movie. <br /><br />It's not a bad book but I found my attention wandering because of already having seen the movie. Had that not been the case,I would have read it all the way through.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26962574" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17258638672896457300">Jaws, Peter Benchley<br/>Jaws is a 1974 novel by American writer Peter Benchley. It tells the story of a great white shark that preys upon a small resort town and the voyage of three men trying to kill it. The novel grows out of Benchley's interest in shark attacks after he learned about the exploits of shark fisherman Frank Mundus in 1964. Doubleday commissioned him to write the novel in 1971, a period when Benchley struggled as a freelance journalist.<br/>تاریخ نخستین خوانش: ماه دسامبر سال 1997 میلادی<br/>عن</span>
  <span id="freeText17258638672896457300" style="display:none">Jaws, Peter Benchley<br />Jaws is a 1974 novel by American writer Peter Benchley. It tells the story of a great white shark that preys upon a small resort town and the voyage of three men trying to kill it. The novel grows out of Benchley's interest in shark attacks after he learned about the exploits of shark fisherman Frank Mundus in 1964. Doubleday commissioned him to write the novel in 1971, a period when Benchley struggled as a freelance journalist.<br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: ماه دسامبر سال 1997 میلادی<br />عنوان: آرواره ها؛ اثر: پیتر بنچلی؛ مترجم: مهرداد فتوره چی؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، جامعه، 1375، در 344، شابک: 9649006524؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان آمریکایی قرن 20 م<br />داستان کوسه ای ست که با نزدیک شدن به آبهای ساحلی شهر «آمیتی» یک زن و بچه ای شناگر را میبلعد. شهر آمیتی بسیار جذاب و محل مناسبی برای شنا كردن است؛ مردمان شهر درآمد خود را از گردشگران به دست میآورند، اما پیدا شدن کوسه ای بزرگ آنهم در فصل تابستان در آبهای ساحلی شهر موجب حوادث ناگواری شده كه ... ا. شربیانی</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1822324039" />
    <a title="Ellen Gail" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27123577-ellen-gail"><img alt="Ellen Gail" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595798462p2/27123577.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1822324039?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellen Gail" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellen Gail" href="/user/show/27123577-ellen-gail">Ellen Gail</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553204650" href="/book/show/941283.Jaws">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27123577-ellen-gail?shelf=read-in-a-day">read-in-a-day</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27123577-ellen-gail?shelf=seen-the-movie">seen-the-movie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27123577-ellen-gail?shelf=stupid-ending">stupid-ending</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1822324039" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8578166945095325567"><b>JAWS</b><br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1509576800ra/24338415.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Peter Benchley writes a mediocre, or average at best, shark story that is fondly remembered by most thanks to Stephen Spielberg. The book is okay, mostly sharky fun weighed down by drama at times, but it can't hold a candle to the greatest shark movie ever made.<br/><br/>So. Truth time. As a small child, think four or five years old, I had a very real fear of being eaten by a shark. We didn't live near water. I was too small to swim and I felt totally safe in the tub. No, these sharks were <i>cartoon sha</i></span>
  <span id="freeText8578166945095325567" style="display:none"><b>JAWS</b><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1509576800ra/24338415.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Peter Benchley writes a mediocre, or average at best, shark story that is fondly remembered by most thanks to Stephen Spielberg. The book is okay, mostly sharky fun weighed down by drama at times, but it can't hold a candle to the greatest shark movie ever made.<br><br>So. Truth time. As a small child, think four or five years old, I had a very real fear of being eaten by a shark. We didn't live near water. I was too small to swim and I felt totally safe in the tub. No, these sharks were <i>cartoon sharks who lived under my bed.</i> They also had giant forks and knives to eat me with.<br><br>I was also scared at one time of the ghost of Abraham Lincoln, who lived in my closet. I was a very imaginative child.<br><br>Who knew as an adult I'd enjoy stories about people being eaten by sharks so much?<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1509576222ra/24338334.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>So, I've already said that this can't hold a candle to the movie adaptation. It just can't, okay? The movie is that good. Had I not seen the movie, I might think better of the book. Maybe. But not by much.<br><br>What drags down the paper and ink version, is the same thing that made the cinema version sing; the shark.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1509576933ra/24338429.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>If you've seen the movie, (and if you haven't I'm going to politely suggest you stop whatever you are doing right now and go watch it,) then you know that it's as much about what you don't see. Even when the shark isn't on screen, the story is busy building suspense.<br><br>This is severely lacking in the suspense department. It has its moments sure, but then there's that dinner party scene where everyone just bitches and gets drunk and there's weird sexual tension and anger. Book-Ellen, is a more complex and developed character than movie-Ellen, I'll give her that. But! She's also terrible.<br><br>Anyway, despite sharing my name, I can't like Ellen Brody. She gets more development, but it doesn't add anything to the story as a whole. It just brings up these weird snippy jealousy issues and I kept wanting to be like, "You know...there's still a shark that's going to eat your tourists."<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1509579717ra/24338710.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>Jaws</i> the book has good moments, but if you've seen the movie I'd say you aren't missing anything. I'll never tell anyone they can't read something, but you've basically already seen the best moments of this made even better. I liked the little details about corruption and the mob, but other than that...*shrug*<br><br>It's no <i>"You'll never go in the water again,"</i> but I'll give it a <i>"You'll consider a vacation to the mountains instead of the beach this year."</i><br><br>Oh wait. The mountains aren't safe either.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1509580308ra/24338753.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Boom! <b>AVALANCHE SHARKS!</b><br><br>In a surprising twist, the only safe place to hide from the myriad of unlikely sharks is UNDER MY CHILDHOOD BED. Where I make an alliance with the hungry cartoon sharks.<br><br>It's Ellen and the UnderbedSharks vs. SyFy Channel Villain Sharks.<br><br>Coming this fall to SyFy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8578166945095325567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_42530607" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/42530607" />
    <a title="Brad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1022982-brad"><img alt="Brad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442484575p2/1022982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/42530607?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad" href="/user/show/1022982-brad">Brad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/941278.Jaws">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=doing-the-dishes">doing-the-dishes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=forty-for-2012-personal-challenge">forty-for-2012-personal-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=in-the-shower">in-the-shower</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=popular-culture">popular-culture</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=hackosaurids">hackosaurids</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=personal-mythology">personal-mythology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42530607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15503413459056614009">Jaws is the tale of a marriage on the edge of failure. Chief Brody, head of the Amity police, is married to Ellen. They've three kids. He's a native of the area; one of the poor boys who spent his days on the beaches while the rich folks came down to vacation from the big cities. She's from one of those big cities, from one of those rich families, and since she married Chief Brody she's been an outsider amongst the natives and outsider amongst the tourists. She belongs nowhere and feels herself </span>
  <span id="freeText15503413459056614009" style="display:none">Jaws is the tale of a marriage on the edge of failure. Chief Brody, head of the Amity police, is married to Ellen. They've three kids. He's a native of the area; one of the poor boys who spent his days on the beaches while the rich folks came down to vacation from the big cities. She's from one of those big cities, from one of those rich families, and since she married Chief Brody she's been an outsider amongst the natives and outsider amongst the tourists. She belongs nowhere and feels herself wasting away in the tiny beach town, and she pines for what once was. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[She ends up sleeping with Matt Hooper, ichthyologist and younger brother of a boy she once loved, much to the Chief's chagrin <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Jaws is the tale of shady land speculation, organized crime and local governmental corruption, wherein another poor local boy "makes good," becomes Mayor, becomes one of the "nouveau riche," then winds up putting lives at risk to save his own skin and pay his bad debts.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ A storyline that parallels and informs what's happening with Ellen, showing us what happens to those moving between classes in either direction (and suggesting that, perhaps, everyone should stay where they fucking belong, amongst their own people -- much to my discomfort and frustration) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Oh yeah ... Jaws is also the tale of a killer shark that starts eating swimmers off the coast of Amity. Chief Brody, Matt Hooper and Quint (the infamous modern Ahab captured so wonderfully by Robert Shaw in Spielberg's movie, although he only shows up in the book in the last eighty pages after one brief half page cameo early on) go out and try to save the people and Amity's economy by catching the greatest of great white sharks. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Hooper dies in this version, and the final take down of the Shark is Quint's rather than Brody's , then Brody swims towards a light house on the coast all by his lonesome. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> It all feels like an afterthought, a tacked on third act of a book that never knew what it wanted to be, and the total lack of closure as the novel ends is pretty disappointing. <br /><br />Once again, the movie proves to be better than the book. Much, much better. <br /><br />Glad I reread this, though. A woman I loved told me to read this again, once upon a time, and I promised I would. It took a decade, but I lived up to the promise.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15503413459056614009" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_154208596" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/154208596" />
    <a title="Andrew Breslin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2716255-andrew-breslin"><img alt="Andrew Breslin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442476483p2/2716255.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/154208596?book_show_action=true">Mar 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew Breslin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew Breslin" href="/user/show/2716255-andrew-breslin">Andrew Breslin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer154208596" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7397391575486361055">Reviewing a book that was made into a movie over 30 years ago and which almost everyone has seen already seems a little pointless. On the other hand I don’t need to post a spoiler alert. If you are somehow unaware of the plot of <i>Jaws</i>, then it doesn’t matter if I give it away, because--I’m sorry to be the one to tell you this--you have Alzheimer’s disease. Yes, it’s a shame. There there. <br/><br/>But even if you already know exactly what is going to happen, this is still a thrilling, chilling read. You sh</span>
  <span id="freeText7397391575486361055" style="display:none">Reviewing a book that was made into a movie over 30 years ago and which almost everyone has seen already seems a little pointless. On the other hand I don’t need to post a spoiler alert. If you are somehow unaware of the plot of <i>Jaws</i>, then it doesn’t matter if I give it away, because--I’m sorry to be the one to tell you this--you have Alzheimer’s disease. Yes, it’s a shame. There there. <br /><br />But even if you already know exactly what is going to happen, this is still a thrilling, chilling read. You should not tackle it while wearing nice pants. In fact, you should probably borrow some Depends from one of those people who doesn’t know what happens at the end. Yeah, a lot of people thought “scare the shit out of you” was just a figure of speech, but who’s scrubbing their drawers with a bucket of undiluted bleach trying to remove that nasty stain now, huh? <br /><br />Assuming you do not have a medical propensity toward incontinence, and are not planning on going swimming in the ocean anytime soon (within the next decade or so), and you want to read a scary, suspenseful thriller, then this is going to be one terrifyingly entertaining treat. Now, for those of you who don’t already know what happens: you have Alzheimer’s. It’s a shame. There there. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7397391575486361055" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3567032496" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3567032496" />
    <a title="Juli" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38617426-juli"><img alt="Juli" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453642808p2/38617426.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3567032496?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Juli" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Juli" href="/user/show/38617426-juli">Juli</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/29540031-jaws">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38617426-juli?shelf=read-audiobooks-2020">read-audiobooks-2020</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3567032496" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11149881601955686341">When I saw this book pop up on Audible+, I had to listen to it! I first read the book my freshman year of high school...and have watched every single movie multiple times (even the horrible 3D one!). There is just something about a monster shark story. I enjoy them all! From Jaws to Sharknado to The Meg -- it's a way for me to encounter something that scares me (being eaten alive is a pretty scary thought!) without actually being in danger. <br/><br/>I enjoyed re-reading the novel that started my love of </span>
  <span id="freeText11149881601955686341" style="display:none">When I saw this book pop up on Audible+, I had to listen to it! I first read the book my freshman year of high school...and have watched every single movie multiple times (even the horrible 3D one!). There is just something about a monster shark story. I enjoy them all! From Jaws to Sharknado to The Meg -- it's a way for me to encounter something that scares me (being eaten alive is a pretty scary thought!) without actually being in danger. <br /><br />I enjoyed re-reading the novel that started my love of Killer Shark tales. Revisiting it as an adult, rather than a 14 year old teen, gave me some new insights into the characters. The Sheriff and his wife's marital issues....an affair based on need, nostalgia and bitterness....small town political drama. I didn't understand most of that when I read this as a kid....but I certainly did this time. And the shark was the same toothy eating machine he has always been. Oh hey look a surfer --- MUNCH! lol<br /><br />The beach and the ocean are two of my favorite places to visit. But, the ocean and the creatures in it are also unpredictable and sometimes frightening. The shark didn't mean it personally ..... it's in its basic nature to hunt and kill. That is its purpose. I can't fault a creature for following its inner programming...but I can avoid it. I still remember vividly a trip to Spain I took with a school group in 1984. We were happily paddle boating and swimming at Torremolinos, when it dawned on me that I couldn't see my feet. We were in deep water. Beautiful blue...the white sand beach in the distance. But......what was swimming near me, under me.....??? I got back in the boat. Immediately. Why? THIS BOOK!! <br /><br />I listened to the audio book version of this story. The audio is just over 9.5 hours long and narrated by Erik Steele. Steele has a pleasant voice and did a great job of bringing the story to life. An entertaining listening experience! I'm now going to watch the movie again. The movie version differs from the book, but the plot is basically intact. They just removed the affair and some of the sub plot to make time for more munching and screaming. The movie has one of the best, and most recognizable, theme songs ever! :) <br /><br />And, I will also admit that thanks to Peter Benchley, I still get nervous when I swim in the ocean and realize I'm in deep enough that I can't see my feet. I always head for shore at that point.... :) <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11149881601955686341" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13885253506521250537">“There's nothing in the sea this fish would fear. Other fish run from bigger things. That's their instinct. But this fish doesn't run from anything. He doesn't fear.” <br/>― Peter Benchley, Jaws<br/><br/><br/>I am not going to do a full review on such a great classic like this.   <span id="freeText13885253506521250537" style="display:none">“There's nothing in the sea this fish would fear. Other fish run from bigger things. That's their instinct. But this fish doesn't run from anything. He doesn't fear.” <br />― Peter Benchley, Jaws<br /><br /><br />I am not going to do a full review on such a great classic like this. But a few thoughts:<br /><br />When this first came out I was but a slip of a tiny child and already reading. Jaws was huge around town. Everyone wanted to read it. My mom would not let me have a copy of it. I could not understand why. I BEGGED her. Eventually we came to a compromise. She would read it to me so it would be less scary and I would get it in small doses.<br /><br />Umm..then she started reading it. And it scared her! And yes me as well.<br /><br />Not as bad as some people. It never did keep me out of the water though we had a friend who became phobic and refused to ever go into the ocean again. I wonder if he ever did..<br /><br />Now I loved the film too but recently I had the chance to see parts of it again and was shocked at just how fake the shark looked. I mean..really fake. I started to laugh. It makes me wonder if I read this again if I would even blink. The things that scare us when we're young...<br /><br />Although that creepy music still does it every time.<br /><br />Anyway..I love this book. End of review.</span>

<div id="review_891051586" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/891051586" />
    <a title="Joe Valdez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez"><img alt="Joe Valdez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585885999p2/26876584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/891051586?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe Valdez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe Valdez" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez">Joe Valdez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/941278.Jaws">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26876584-joe-valdez?shelf=sci-fi-general">sci-fi-general</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer891051586&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer891051586" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4793477658305711792">The problem with Peter Benchley's 1974 debut novel <i>Jaws</i> is that there's a film adaptation -- a troubled production that exploded into theaters a year later and changed the way Hollywood distributed films forever -- and it's one of the greatest films ever made, on par with <i>Vertigo</i> and <i>Psycho</i> for my money. The source material, on the other hand, is not one of the greatest novels ever written. It's not in the ballpark and isn't even the same sport. <br/><br/>In his 2005 introduction with this edition, Benchl</span>
  <span id="freeText4793477658305711792" style="display:none">The problem with Peter Benchley's 1974 debut novel <i>Jaws</i> is that there's a film adaptation -- a troubled production that exploded into theaters a year later and changed the way Hollywood distributed films forever -- and it's one of the greatest films ever made, on par with <i>Vertigo</i> and <i>Psycho</i> for my money. The source material, on the other hand, is not one of the greatest novels ever written. It's not in the ballpark and isn't even the same sport. <br /><br />In his 2005 introduction with this edition, Benchley sheepishly addresses the differences between his bestseller and the classic movie, as if one too many party guests had stared into their drinks over the years and admitted, "I read <i>Jaws</i> after I saw the movie. Your novel was, different." The book might be a four-star or five-star affair for those who still haven't seen the movie, teenagers maybe, and two stars or less for those like me who grew up with the beloved film.<br /><br />Similarities between book and film:<br /><br />A 20-foot great white shark does terrorize the bathers of the fictional resort of Amity Island. There is a police chief named Martin Brody who hates the water. He has a wife and two boys. There's a marine biologist named Matt Hooper (more of a Robert Redford type in the book) and a mariner named Quint who ultimately go in search of the beast with Brody. There's an attack on a female skinnydipper (the first and best scene of the book) as well as the boy Alex Kittner on his raft, both memorable set pieces in the movie. The shady Mayor Vaughan, timed perfectly as the Watergate coverup was bringing down the Nixon administration, is featured in the book as well.<br /><br />I really don't like to nitpick every change that was made between a book and a movie, but it bares mention how far Benchley wanders off the reservation with material that doesn't strengthen his concept in any way. Many of Benchley's decisions detract from and weaken his concept. <br /><br />The biggest boner is the character of Ellen Brody, whom Benchley devotes more copy to than <i>any other character in the story</i>. There's a police chief whose voters are being eaten by a killer shark, a marine biologist who studies killer sharks and a crusty sailor who hunts killer sharks, as well as a killer shark, but the character Benchley was really interested in is a housewife who has <i>nothing to do with the killer shark</i>.<br /><br />In the book, Ellen throws a dinner party for Hooper (while a killer shark is on the loose), sneaks off for lunch with Hooper (while a killer shark is on the loose) and bangs Hooper (while a killer shark is on the loose). Keep in mind that the title of the book is not <i>Ellen Brody</i>. It was never, to my knowledge, promoted as literary fiction. John Updike is not the author. The title is <i>Jaws</i> and this is supposed to be about the mysterious appearance of a killer shark and how it terrorizes a town. Benchley, who was retained to write the first draft of the screenplay, admits his marching orders from producers David Brown &amp; Richard D. Zanuck was to cut anything not related to the shark. Where Benchley's editors were with Creative Writing 101 advice this basic is beyond me.<br /><br />At least shark two attacks in the book are powerful, more vivid than those in the movie. Steven Spielberg had a mechanical shark that didn't work and as a result, couldn't really show audiences what it was up to. Benchley's imagination had no such constraints, but a major weakness of the book is that when the shark isn't attacking, there's absolutely no terror or suspense. Benchley barely acknowledges Brody's sons, who like most of the town, seem more annoyed and perplexed by the existence of the shark than in mortal fear of it.<br /><br />The climax of the book isn't just inferior to the film, it's a poor excuse for an ending to a thriller. <br /><br />I think it's possible that Benchley and his editors knew his concept was so strong and such a cash cow that the novel didn't need to work. But without compelling characters or dialogue, pacing that draws the reader from scene into the next, or more than just token thrills, the concept doesn't mean much to me. I did finish the book, in one long Labor Day nestled in Laguna Beach on the Pacific Coast. I can recommend the beach, but not the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4793477658305711792" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/59542.Peter_Benchley">About Peter Benchley</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/59542.Peter_Benchley"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1343794280p3/59542.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/59542.Peter_Benchley">Peter Benchley
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
618
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9267227463870718561">Peter Bradford Benchley was an American author best known for writing the novel <i>Jaws</i> and co-writing the screenplay for its highly successful film adaptation. The success of the book led to many publishers commissioning books about mutant rats, rabid dogs and the like threatening communities. The subsequent film directed by Steven Spielberg and co-written by Benchley is generally acknowledged as th</span>
  <span id="freeText9267227463870718561" style="display:none">Peter Bradford Benchley was an American author best known for writing the novel <i>Jaws</i> and co-writing the screenplay for its highly successful film adaptation. The success of the book led to many publishers commissioning books about mutant rats, rabid dogs and the like threatening communities. The subsequent film directed by Steven Spielberg and co-written by Benchley is generally acknowledged as the first summer blockbuster. Benchley also wrote <i>The Deep</i> and <i>The Island</i> which were also adapted into films.<br /><br />Benchley was from a literary family. He was the son of author Nathaniel Benchley and grandson of Algonquin Round Table founder Robert Benchley. His younger brother, Nat Benchley, is a writer and actor. Peter Benchley was an alumnus of Phillips Exeter Academy and Harvard University.<br /><br />After graduating from college, he worked for <i>The Washington Post</i>, then as an editor at <i>Newsweek</i> and a speechwriter in the White House. He developed the idea of a man-eating shark terrorising a community after reading of a fisherman Frank Mundus catching a 4,550 pound great white shark off the coast of Long Island in 1964. He also drew some material from the tragic Jersey Shore shark attacks of 1916.<br /><br />His reasonably successful second novel, <i>The Deep</i>, is about a honeymooning couple discovering two sunken treasures on the Bermuda reefs—17th century Spanish gold and a fortune in World War Two-era morphine—who are subsequently targeted by a drug syndicate. This 1976 novel is based on Benchley's chance meeting in Bermuda with diver Teddy Tucker while writing a story for <i>National Geographic</i>. Benchley co-wrote the screenplay for the 1977 film release, along with Tracy Keenan Wynn and an uncredited Tom Mankiewicz. Directed by Peter Yates and starring Robert Shaw, Nick Nolte and Jacqueline Bisset, <i>The Deep</i> was the second-highest grossing release of 1977 after <i>Star Wars</i>, although its box office tally fell well short of <i>Jaws</i>.<br /><br /><i>The Island</i>, published in 1979, was a story of descendants of 17th century pirates who terrorize pleasure craft in the Caribbean, leading to the Bermuda Triangle mystery. Benchley again wrote the screenplay for the film adaptation. But the movie version of <i>The Island</i>, starring Michael Caine and David Warner, failed at the box office when released in 1980.<br /><br />During the 1980s, Benchley wrote three novels that did not sell as well as his previous works. However, <i>Girl of the Sea of Cortez</i>, a beguiling John Steinbeck-type fable about man's complicated relationship with the sea, was far and away his best reviewed book and has attracted a considerable cult following since its publication. <i>Sea of Cortez</i> signposted Benchley's growing interest in ecological issues and anticipated his future role as an impassioned and intelligent defender of the importance of redressing the current imbalance between human activities and the marine environment. <i>Q Clearance</i> published in 1986 was written from his experience as a staffer in the Johnson White House. <i>Rummies</i> (aka <i>Lush</i>), which appeared in 1989, is a semi-autobiographical work, loosely inspired by the Benchley family's history of alcohol abuse. While the first half of the novel is a relatively straightforward (and harrowing) account of a suburbanite's descent into alcoholic hell, the second part—which takes place at a New Mexico substance abuse clinic—veers off into wildly improbable thriller-type territory.<br /><br />He returned to nautical themes in 1991's <i>Beast</i> written about a giant squid threatening Bermuda. <i>Beast</i> was brought to the small screen as a made-for-TV movie in 1996, under the slightly altered title <i>The Beast</i>. His next novel, <i>White Shark</i>, was published in 1994. The story of a Nazi-created genetically engineered shark/human hybrid failed to achieve popular or critical success.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9267227463870718561" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
